فان ابوا استعانوا بالله تعالى عليهم وحاربوهم ونصبوا عليهم المجانيق وحرقوهم وارسلوا عليهم الماء وقطعوا اشجارهم وافسدوا زروعهم [۲۹۸۸] (۹) پس اگر انکار کرے تو اللہ سے مدد مانگ کر ان سے لڑائی کریں۔ اور ان پر منجنیقین لگا ئیں اور ان کو جلائیں اور ان پر پانی چھوڑ دیں اور ان کے درختوں کو کاٹ ڈالیں اور ان کی کھیتیاں اجاڑ دیں۔ جزیہ دینے سے بھی انکار کر دیں تو ان سے قتال کیا جائے گا۔ اور قتال کرنے کی مختلف صورتیں ہیں جو اس زمانے میں رائج تھیں۔ مثلا ان پر منجنیق نصب کریں، ان کے گھروں کو آگ لگادیں، ان پر گرم پانی چھوڑ دیں تا کہ وہ مرجائیں یا مجبور ہوکر ہتھیار ڈال دیں۔ ان کے درختوں کو کاٹ دیں۔ اور ان کی کھیتیوں کو اجاڑ دیں تا کہ وہ مجبور ہو جائے۔ جزیہ نہ دینے پر قتال کریں اس کی دلیل اوپر کی حدیث میں گزر چکی ہے۔ عن سليمان بن بريدة عن ابيه ... فان ابوا فاستعن بالله وقاتلهم (الف) (مسلم شریف، باب تا میرا الامام الامراء علی البحوث، ج ۲، ص۸۲ نمبر ۷۳ امر ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی دعاء المشرکین، ص ۱۳۶۱ نمبر ۲۶۱۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ جزیہ دینے کے لئے بھی تیار نہ ہو تو اللہ کا نام لیکر قتال شروع کرے۔ اس کے لئے منجنیق یا اس زمانے کا اٹم بم استعمال کرے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابی عبید ان رسول الله الله حاصر اهل الطائف و نصب عليهم المنجنيق سبعة عشر يوما (ب) (سن ليبقى ، باب قطع الشجر حرق المنازل، ج تاسع ص ۱۴۴، نمبر ۱۸۱۲۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ان پر منجنیق نصب کرے۔ جلانے کی حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن ابن عمر قال حرق رسول الله الا الله نخل بني النضير وقطع وهي البويرة فنزل ما قطعتم من لينة او تركتموها قائمة على اصولها فباذن الله وليخزى الفاسقين (ج) ( آیت ۵ ،سورۃ الحشر ۵۹ر بخاری شریف، باب حديث بنى التفسير ومخرج رسول الله الله السهم في دبيت الرجلين ، ص ۵۷۴، نمبر ۴۰۳۱ ، مسلم شریف ، باب جواز قطع اشجار الكفار وتحر یقھا ، ج ۲، ص ۸۵ نمبر ۱۷۴۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کفار کے گھروں کو جلا سکتے ہیں۔ اور اس کے درختوں کو کاٹ سکتے ہیں اگر ضرورت پڑ جائے تو ۔ اس حدیث میں درخت کاٹنے اور کھیتی بر باد کرنے کی بھی دلیل ہے۔ کفار پر پانی چھوڑے یا اس کا پانی بند کرے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن علی قال امرنی رسول الله الله ان اغور ماء آبار بدر (و) (سن للمقی ، باب قطع الشجر وحرق المنازل، ج تاسع ص ۱۲۵ نمبر ۱۸۱۲۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کفار کا پانی بند کرے اور ضرورت پڑے تو گرم پانی اس پر ڈالے تا کہ میدان جنگ سے بھاگ جائے۔ حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت سلیمان کی حدیث میں ہے ... پس اگر کفار جزیہ دینے سے بھی انکار کرے تو اللہ سے مدد مانگو اور ان سے جنگ کرو (ب ) آپ نے اہل طائف کا محاصرہ کیا اور ان پر سترہ دنوں تک منجنیق متعین کئے رکھا (ج) آپ نے بنی تفسیر کے باغ کو جلایا اور اس کو کاٹا جس کا نام ہی یرہ تھا۔ جس کی بنا پر یہ آیت نازل ہوئی۔ جو تم تر درخت کاٹو یا اس کو تنے پر چھوڑ دو تو اللہ کے حکم سے ہے۔ اور فاسقوں کو ذلیل کرنے کے لئے ہے (د) حضرت علی نے فرمایا کہ حضور نے مجھے حکم دیا کہ بدر کے پانی کو نیچا تاردوں۔ وحرقوهم وارسلوا عليهم الماء وقطعوا اشجارهم وافسدوا زروعهم [۲۹۸۹] (۱۰) ...... یہ سب کام ضرورت پڑنے پر کرے ورنہ اس کی ضرورت نہ ہو تو ان کے پھل دار درختوں کو نہ کاٹے اور نہ کھیتیوں کو برباد کرے۔ حضرت ابو بکر کی وصیت میں ہے۔ ان ابا بكر لما بعث الجنود نحو الشام يزيد بن ابی سفیان وعمرو بن العاص وشرحبيل بن حسنة ... وان هم ابوا فاستعينوا بالله عليهم فقاتلوهم ! ان شاء الله ولا تغرقن نخلا ولا تحرقنها ولا تعقروا بهيمة ولا شجرة تشمر ولا تهدموا بيعة ولا تقتلوا الولدان و لا الشيوخ ولا النساء وستجدون اقواما حبسوا انفسهم في الصوامع فدعوهم وما حبسوا انفسهم له وستجدون آخرين اتخذوا الشيطان في رؤوسهم افحاصا فاذا وجدتم اولئك فاضربوا اعناقهم ان شاء الله (الف) ( سنن للبیہقی ، باب من اختار الكف عن القطع والتحريق اذا كان الاغلب انها تقصیر دار اسلام او دار عهد ، ج تاسع میں ۱۴۶ نمبر ۱۸۱۲۵ر موطا امام مالک، باب انھی عن قتل النساء والوالدان في الغزو ص ۴۶۵ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ بلاضرورت نہ پھل دار درخت کاٹے اور نہ کھیتی اجاڑے۔ مجانیق : منجنیق کی جمع ہے، پچھلے زمانے میں گولہ پھینکنے کا آلہ تھا اسکو میزائل کہنے لگے ہیں۔ ارسل : چھوڑنا پھینکنا۔
If they refuse, then seek help from Allah against them and fight them, and set up mangonels against them, and burn them, and release water upon them, and cut their trees, and spoil their crops. (9) Then if they refuse, seek help from Allah and fight them. Set up mangonels against them, burn them, release water upon them, cut their trees, and spoil their crops. If they also refuse to pay jizya, fighting will be done against them. There are various forms of fighting prevalent at that time: set up mangonels against them, set their houses on fire, release hot water upon them so that they die or are forced to surrender, cut their trees, and spoil their crops so that they are compelled. The evidence for fighting upon refusal of jizya has passed in the earlier hadith from Sulayman ibn Burayda from his father: 'If they refuse, then seek help from Allah and fight them.' (Muslim, Abu Dawud). From this hadith it is understood that if they are not ready to pay jizya, then begin fighting in the name of Allah. The evidence for using mangonel or modern bomb is the hadith from Abu Ubayd that the Messenger of Allah besieged the people of Taif and set up a mangonel against them for seventeen days (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). The hadith for burning is from Ibn Umar: The Messenger of Allah burned the date palms of Banu al-Nadir and cut them down, and they were at Buwayra. Then the verse was revealed: 'Whatever you cut down of the palm trees or left them standing on their trunks, it was by Allah's permission and to disgrace the wicked.' (Surah al-Hashr:5, Bukhari, Muslim). This hadith shows that the houses of disbelievers can be burned and their trees cut if necessary. The hadith for releasing water or blocking water is from Ali: The Messenger of Allah commanded me to lower the water of the wells of Badr (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). From this it is understood that the water of disbelievers can be blocked and if needed, hot water can be poured on them so they flee the battlefield. Footnote: (a) In the hadith of Sulayman: If the disbelievers refuse to pay jizya, seek help from Allah and fight them. (b) He besieged the people of Taif and set up mangonels against them for seventeen days. (c) He burned the garden of Banu al-Nadir and cut it, which was called Buwayra, and this verse was revealed. (d) Ali said that the Prophet commanded him to lower the water of Badr. And they burned them, and released water upon them, and cut their trees, and spoiled their crops. (10) All these actions are done when necessary; otherwise, if there is no need, do not cut their fruit-bearing trees nor spoil their crops. In the will of Abu Bakr: When he sent armies towards Sham under Yazid ibn Abi Sufyan, Amr ibn al-As, and Shurahbil ibn Hasana... and if they refuse, then seek help from Allah against them and fight them, if Allah wills. Do not drown date palms, nor burn them, nor disable any beast, nor cut any fruit-bearing tree, nor destroy any church, nor kill children, old men, or women. You will find people who have confined themselves in monasteries; leave them with their confinement. You will find others who have made Satan the crown of their heads; when you find them, strike their necks, if Allah wills. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Muwatta Imam Malik). From this effect it is understood that without necessity, do not cut fruit-bearing trees nor spoil crops. Majaniq: plural of manjanik, a device for throwing projectiles in ancient times, now called missiles. Arsal: release, throw.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Siyar (Jihad) presents the ruling that when non-Muslims refuse to pay jizya after being called to Islam, Muslims are permitted to wage war and use necessary force. The author lists specific destructive measures: setting up mangonels (siege engines), burning houses, releasing water to flood or scald, cutting down trees, and destroying crops. These were common military tactics at the time. The author then provides supporting hadith evidence: the hadith of Sulayman ibn Burayda commands fighting those who refuse jizya; the hadith of the siege of Taif shows the use of mangonels; the hadith of Ibn Umar shows the burning of palm trees was sanctioned by revelation; the hadith of Ali shows blocking or spoiling water sources. The author adds a footnote explaining each hadith. Importantly, the author clarifies that these measures are only permissible out of military necessity. He cites the instruction of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq to his armies, which prohibits wanton destruction of fruit trees, churches, killing of non-combatants (women, children, elderly), and animal mutilation. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that destruction is permitted only to compel the enemy to surrender, not for mere vengeance. The commentator also explains the term 'majaniq' (mangonels) as ancient projectile weapons, analogous to modern missiles.

Key Terms
منجنيق (manjanīq): A siege engine for throwing projectiles, like a mangonel or trebuchet; used in ancient warfare.
جزيہ (jizyah): A tax paid by non-Muslim subjects (dhimmi) in an Islamic state in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi ruling on burning enemy crops during jihad?
A) It is always permissible as a standard military tactic.
B) It is never permissible because property destruction is prohibited.
C) It is permissible only when necessary to force the enemy to surrender, and even then must avoid unnecessary harm.
D) It is permissible only if the enemy commits atrocities first.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits destroying enemy property during jihad only when military necessity demands it, and even then with a presumption against harming productive resources and non-combatants.

Shooting When Enemy Uses Muslim Shields
Ruling on attacking despite Muslim captives as shields

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attacking when the enemy uses Muslim human shields and explain the evidence from the hadith of Mus'ab.

Core Idea
It is permissible to attack even if Muslim shields are used, as long as the intention is to hit the enemy.

This passage addresses a difficult battlefield scenario: the enemy using Muslim prisoners or children as shields. The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith, permits continued attack with proper intention. This lesson examines the ruling, its conditions, and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? It is shooting at the enemy when they have placed Muslim prisoners, merchants, or children in front. The ruling is permissible (mubah).
The text begins with 'ولا بأس' which indicates permissibility.
2
Locate the dalil
What is the proof? The hadith of Mus'ab ibn Jaththama where the Prophet was asked about a night raid on polytheists' houses that strikes women and children. He replied, 'They are of them.'
The hadith is cited in the footnote.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The phrase 'هم منهم' indicates that women and children present with the enemy are considered part of the enemy group, so collateral damage is not sinful.
The intention must be to strike the enemy, not the non-combatants.
4
Note the condition
What condition must be met? The Muslims must intend to hit the disbelievers, not the shields. If they intend the Muslims, it would be sinful.
The text emphasizes 'لم يقصدوا المسلمين'.
5
Consider the context
When does this ruling apply? When the enemy has overpowered the Muslims and placed them in front such that Muslims cannot target them separately.
The ruling assumes military necessity; if possible to avoid hitting shields, one must do so.

Source Text

ولا بأس برميهم وان كان فيهم مسلم اسير او تاجر وان تترسوا بصبيان المسلمين او قتلوا نفس هؤلاء الذين تترسوا بهم ان المسلمين اذا رموا لم يقصدوا المسلمين وانما يقصدون الكفار وإن اصاب المسلم اسيرا او تاجرا او صبيا او امراة فلا اثم عليهم لقصدهم الكفار دونهم ولا يثبت لهم حكم التترس ان يكونوا مستولي الكفار على المسلمين وهم وجعلوهم امامهم حتى لا تتمكن المسلمين من رميهم فيجوز للمسلمين ان يرميهم وني ولم يقصدوا المسلمين . حاشية : حديث عن المصعب بن جثامة قال . النبى الله بالابواء او بودان فسئل عن اهل الدار يبيتون من المشركين فيصاب من نسائهم وذراريهم ؟ قال هم منهم (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب اہل الدار بیتون فیصاب حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابو بکڑ نے لشکر شام کی طرف بھیجا۔ اور یہ فرمایا اگروہ جزیہ سے بھی انکار کرے تو اللہ سے مدد مانگو اور ان شاء اللہ ان سے جنگ کرو ۔ درختوں کو پانی میں نہ ڈباؤ اور نہ اس کو جلاؤ۔ اور نہ جانور کے پاؤں کاٹو اور نہ پھل دار درخت کاٹو۔ اور نہ گر جاؤں کو گراؤ اور نہ بچوں کو قتل کرو اور نہ بوڑھوں کو اور نہ عورتوں کو قتل کرو۔ ایسے لوگ بھی ملیں گے جنہوں نے اپنے آپ کو گر جاؤں میں محبوس کر رکھا ہے ان کو اسی حال پر چھوڑ دو ۔ کچھ ایسے لوگ پائیں گے کہ شیطان کو اپنا سر دار بنا رکھا ہے۔ ایسے لوگ ملیں تو اگر مناسب ہو تو ان کی گردنیں اڑا دو (ب) مصعب بن جامہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مقام ابواء یا مقام باوران میں حضور میرے سامنے سے گزرے تو مشرکین کے گھروں میں جو لوگ عورتیں اور بچے رہتے ہیں ان کے بارے میں پوچھا۔ تو آپ نے فرمایا ان کا شمار مشرکین میں سے ہے۔
And there is no harm in shooting them even if there is among them a Muslim prisoner or a trader, and even if they take Muslim children as shields. If Muslims shoot, they do not intend the Muslims but only intend the disbelievers, even if a Muslim prisoner, trader, child, or woman is hit, there is no sin upon them because they intended the disbelievers, not them. The ruling of using shields (tatarruq) is established when the disbelievers have overpowered Muslims and placed them in front so that Muslims cannot shoot them. In that case, it is permissible for Muslims to shoot intending the disbelievers, not the Muslims. Footnote: Hadith from Mus'ab ibn Jaththama: The Prophet was at al-Abwa or Waddan and was asked about the people of a house who are attacked at night from the polytheists and some of their women and children are struck. He said: 'They are of them.' (Bukhari). (b) Mus'ab ibn Jaththama said: The Prophet passed by me at al-Abwa or Waddan, and he was asked about the houses of polytheists in which women and children live, and he said: 'They are counted among the polytheists.'
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage addresses a difficult situation in warfare: when the enemy uses Muslim prisoners, merchants, or children as human shields to prevent Muslims from attacking. The author rules that it is permissible for Muslims to continue shooting at the enemy, even if it might result in the death of those Muslim shields. The key condition is that the Muslims' intention must be to hit the disbelievers, not the Muslims. If a Muslim is accidentally killed, there is no sin on the attacker because the intent was not to kill him. The author then cites a hadith from Mus'ab ibn Jaththama where the Prophet was asked about a night raid that kills women and children of polytheists, and he replied, 'They are of them' (i.e., they are counted as polytheists). This indicates that in a legitimate military attack, collateral casualties among non-combatants who are mixed with the enemy are not sinful if the intent was to engage the combatants. However, the author also implies that this permission is only when the Muslims are unable to target the enemy without hitting the shields. The footnote provides additional context: the hadith of Mus'ab is about a situation where women and children are present in the enemy's houses during a raid. The Hanafi position is that such casualties are regrettable but not sinful if unavoidable. This ruling balances the obligation to fight the enemy with the protection of Muslim lives, prioritizing the collective military necessity over individual lives when unavoidable.

Key Terms
تترس (tatarruṣ): The act of using human shields; when the enemy places Muslims in front to protect themselves.
اسیر (asīr): A prisoner of war; in this context, a Muslim who is captured and used as a shield.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the condition for permissibility of shooting when Muslim prisoners are used as shields?
A) The prisoners must consent to being sacrificed.
B) The Muslims must intend to kill the enemy, not the Muslims.
C) There must be no other way to attack the enemy.
D) The enemy must first kill some of the prisoners.

Key Takeaway
It is permissible to attack the enemy even when Muslim human shields are used, provided the attacker intends the enemy and not the shields.

Destroying Enemy Property and Shooting at Human Shields
States two rulings on warfare conduct with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on destroying enemy property and shooting at Muslim captives used as shields, and identify the proofs from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Necessity permits collateral harm in warfare as long as intention targets the enemy, not Muslims.

Warfare involves difficult choices between military necessity and protecting life and property. The Hanafi school addresses two such scenarios: destroying enemy resources and attacking when Muslims are used as shields. This lesson unpacks the rulings and the prophetic proofs that balance necessity with intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rulings
Read the passage and list the two acts being permitted: (1) destroying enemy property (burning, flooding, cutting trees, spoiling crops) and (2) shooting at the enemy even when Muslim prisoners or children are present as shields.
The text explicitly states 'there is no harm in shooting at them' despite the presence of Muslims.
2
Locate the Proofs
Note the two types of proofs: the instruction of Abu Bakr to his generals (a qawl al-ṣaḥābī) and the hadiths of the Prophet (peace be upon him) on collateral harm during night raids.
Abu Bakr's instructions include prohibitions on killing non-combatants but also imply permissible destruction of property in context.
3
Understand the Legal Reasoning (Wajh al-Dalalah)
Analyze how the proofs yield the rulings. The imperative 'do not drown palm trees' is a general prohibition, but the context of warfare and the principle of necessity (ḍarūrah) allow exceptions. The hadiths show that unintended collateral harm to women and children is excused when the attack is directed at combatants.
The Hanafi school uses istiḥsān to prioritize necessity over literal prohibition.
4
Note the Intention Requirement
The passage emphasizes that in shooting, the intention must be to hit the disbelievers, not the Muslims. This condition distinguishes permissible collateral harm from intentional murder.
The hadith explicitly says 'Do not intend them, and there is no harm.'
5
Compare with Other Schools
Recognize that this is a disputed matter (mukhtalaf fīh). Some other schools restrict destruction of property more strictly and may forbid shooting at human shields absolutely.
The Hanafi position weighs overall military benefit against individual harm.

And burn them and send water upon them and cut their trees and spoil their crops. [2989(10)] There is no harm in shooting at them even if among them there is a Muslim prisoner or a merchant, and if they shield themselves with Muslim children or prisoners, they do not stop shooting at them, and they intend by shooting the disbelievers not the Muslims. Indeed, Abu Bakr when he sent the armies towards Sham: Yazid ibn Abi Sufyan, Amr ibn al-As, and Shurahbil ibn Hasanah... and if they refuse, then seek help from Allah against them and fight them, if Allah wills. And do not drown a palm tree nor burn it, nor cripple an animal, nor cut a fruitful tree, nor demolish a church, nor kill children, old men, or women. And you will find people who have confined themselves in monasteries, leave them to what they have confined themselves for. And you will find others who have taken Satan as their leader, when you find them, strike their necks if Allah wills. He said: They are from them. From Ibn Umar, he said: I found a woman killed in some of the battles of the Messenger of Allah, then the Messenger of Allah forbade killing women and children. He said: O Messenger of Allah, the children of the polytheists, do we hit them in a night raid? He said: Do not intend them, and there is no harm, he said: Their children are from them.

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the Hanafi school on warfare conduct, supported by proofs. The first ruling allows burning, flooding, cutting trees, and spoiling crops of the enemy when necessary, as shown by the instruction of Abu Bakr to his generals. The second ruling permits shooting at the enemy even if Muslim prisoners or merchants are among them, and even if they use Muslim children or prisoners as shields, as long as the intention is to hit the disbelievers. This is supported by hadiths that allow collateral harm when unavoidable, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said that women and children are considered 'from them' in such attacks. The Hanafi school emphasizes necessity and intention: one does not intend to harm Muslims but focuses on the enemy.

Key Terms
تترسوا (tatarrasū): To shield themselves, making a human shield.
رمي (ramy): Shooting (arrows or projectiles) at the enemy.
أسارى (asārā): Plural of asīr, meaning prisoners of war.

The Hanafi school permits destruction of enemy property and shooting at human shields when necessary, provided the intention is against the enemy, not Muslims.

Ruling on Taking Women and Qurans with the Army
States the ruling on women and Qurans accompanying the army with a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking women and Qurans with the army and explain the condition that changes the ruling from permissible to disliked.

Core Idea
Taking women and Qurans to war is permissible only when the army is large enough to ensure safety.

In jihad, the safety of vulnerable individuals and sacred texts must be weighed against military needs. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: it is permissible to take women and children (and copies of the Quran) when the army is strong enough to protect them. This lesson examines the textual basis and the condition that makes the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking women and Qurans out with the Muslim army. The ruling is that there is 'no harm' (la ba'sa), i.e., permissible, but with a condition.
The term 'la ba'sa' in Hanafi fiqh usually indicates permissibility (ibahah).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade traveling with the Quran to enemy lands.
This hadith is reported in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition is linked to the risk of desecration (hatk hurma). By analogy, women are similarly protected from harm. However, the prohibition is not absolute; it is conditional upon fear of desecration.
The Hanafi school uses the principle that rulings follow the effective cause (illah). When the cause is absent, the ruling changes.
4
Note the condition
The text adds the condition: 'if they are a large army (askaran azima), safe for the children and offspring.' This means if the army is large enough to guarantee protection, the prohibition is lifted.
A large army is one that can defend itself and its dependents from enemy capture.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is permissible for a large army and disliked (makruh tanzihi) for a small army where safety is not assured.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position of the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۹۰](۱۱) ولا بأس باخراج النساء والمصاحف مع المسلمين اذا كانوا عسكرا عظيما يؤمن عليه الولدان والذراری. عن عبد الله بن عمر ان رسول الله الا الله نهى ان يسافر بالقرآن الى ارض العدو.
[2990](11) There is no harm in taking out women and Qurans with the Muslims if they are a large army, safe for the children and offspring. From Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade traveling with the Quran to the land of the enemy.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on the permissibility of taking women and Qurans along with the Muslim army. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a large army (where safety is assured) and a small expedition (where the risk of capture and desecration is high). For a large army, it is allowed; for a small contingent, it is disliked (makruh). The proof comes from the hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet forbade traveling with the Quran to enemy lands for fear of desecration. The same logic applies to women, to protect their honor.

Key Terms
مصاحف (maṣāḥif): Plural of muṣḥaf, meaning copies of the Quran.
عسكرا عظيما (ʿaskaran ʿaẓīman): A large army, secure enough to protect women and children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only when the army is small and fast.
B) When the army is large and safety is assured.
C) It is always forbidden.
D) Only if the enemy agrees.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of taking women and Qurans on a military expedition hinges on the army's strength and the safety of the dependents.

The Prohibition of Intentionally Killing Non-Combatants in War
Explains prohibition and exception via hadith and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intentionally killing women and children in war, cite the supporting hadith, and distinguish between intentional and accidental killing.

Core Idea
Intentionally killing women and children in war is haram; accidental casualties are excused.

This lesson examines the Islamic rules of engagement regarding non-combatants. The Hanafi school, based on clear prophetic prohibitions, holds that women and children must not be targeted deliberately. However, accidental casualties during legitimate military operations are not sinful. We will explore the textual evidence and the legal reasoning from the source passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and what the hukm level is. The act is intentionally killing women and children of the enemy during war. The ruling is haram (prohibited).
The prohibition is explicit from the hadith.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the evidence cited. The author cites the hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade killing women and children (Bukhari). Also the Tabarani narration with the phrase 'la ta'ammadu dhalik' (do not intentionally do that).
Both hadith are ahad (single-transmitter), so thubut is zanni.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The imperative form 'la ta'ammadu' is a nahy indicating prohibition (haram). The exception 'wa la haraj' (no sin) for accidental hitting shows the prohibition is for intentional acts only.
Hanafi usul: al-nahy yufidu al-tahrim.
4
Apply qiyas
The author extends the ruling by analogy: if Muslim children or captives are unintentionally hit, there is also no sin. The illa is the absence of intent to kill non-combatants.
Qiyas is used to derive rulings for similar cases not explicitly mentioned.
5
Note the exception for attacking women
The text states 'if a woman attacks, then she will be killed.' This is an exception based on active combatant status.
The general prohibition covers non-combatants; those who directly engage lose protection.

Source Text

Regarding captives, if they do not stop shooting, and they aim with shooting at the disbelievers, not the Muslims. And there is no harm in taking out women and Qur'ans with the Muslims if they are a large army in which the children and dependents are safe. And in Tabarani's narration this sentence is additional: 'He said: O Messenger of Allah, the children of the polytheists, do we attack them in a night raid? He said: Do not intentionally do that, and there is no sin. He said: Their children are among them.' From this hadith it is understood: Do not intentionally kill the children and women of the polytheists, but if they get hit in between, there is no problem. And by analogy on this, if Muslim children and captives are hit by an arrow, there is no problem. However, one should not throw an arrow intending them. Killing the women and children of disbelievers without reason is prohibited; the evidence for this is in this hadith: 'Ibn Umar said: I found a woman killed in some of the battles of the Messenger of Allah, so the Messenger of Allah prohibited killing women and children.' Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding the rules of engagement in war. It begins by stating the ruling: if captives are in the line of fire and do not stop shooting, the Muslims should aim only at the disbelievers, not at the captives. Then it moves to the permissibility of taking women and Qur'an on a military campaign when the army is large enough that they are safe. The author then cites a hadith from Tabarani where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbids intentionally killing children of polytheists in night raids, but if they are accidentally hit, there is no sin. This is extended by analogy to Muslim children and captives. The author then provides another hadith from Bukhari where the Prophet expressly prohibits killing women and children. An exception is made if a woman attacks, then she may be killed. The passage also includes a narration from Abu Bakr with a similar prohibition. Finally, the author explains key Arabic terms: 'tatarus' (using shields), 'asara' (captives), 'lam yakfu' (they do not stop), and 'rami' (throwing arrows). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi position that non-combatants are protected, but accidental casualties are excused.

رمى (rami): Shooting arrows or throwing projectiles.
تترسوا (tatarusu): To use people as shields (from turs, shield).

The Hanafi Ruling: Women and Qur'an on Military Campaigns
Differentiates between large and small armies regarding taking women and Qur'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for taking women and copies of the Qur'an on military campaigns, differentiate between large and small armies, and apply the relevant hadith proof to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Taking women and Qur'an on campaign is permissible when security is assured; prohibition is conditional on fear of desecration.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in the chapter of jihad: when is it permissible to take women and copies of the Qur'an along with the army? The Hanafi school draws a clear distinction based on the size and security of the force, balancing military necessity with the need to protect honor and sanctity. We will examine the foundational hadith evidence and understand how the school derives a conditional ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Identify the act being ruled upon: taking women (women) and masahif (copies of the Qur'an) on a military campaign. State the two-part ruling: mubah (permissible) when the army is large and secure; makruh tanzihi (disliked) when the army is small and vulnerable.
The term 'sariyah' refers to a small detachment; 'jaysh' refers to a large army.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the primary proof: the hadith from Ibn Umar (RA) prohibiting traveling with the Qur'an to enemy lands, recorded in Bukhari and Muslim. Note that another narration adds the reason: 'fear that the enemy might dishonor it' (makhafata an yanalahu al-'aduww).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in fiqh rulings.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
Understand how the Hanafi school interprets the prohibition. The explicit reason indicates that the prohibition is conditional on risk. When security is assured (large army), the rationale is absent, so the prohibition lifts, and the act becomes permissible. The prohibition is understood as karahah (dislike) when risk remains.
This is an application of the usul principle: al-hukm yaduru ma'a illatihi wujudan wa 'adaman.
4
Note the Practical Differentiation
Apply the ruling to concrete scenarios: a large, well-equipped army (jaysh) that is confident of victory can take women and Qur'an without dislike. A small raiding party (sariyah) at risk of being overwhelmed should avoid taking them, as it is makruh.
The same logic applies to modern military contexts: deploy religious texts only when secure.

Source Text

There is no harm in taking women and the Qur'an with the Muslims when they are a large army and they are secure. And taking them is disliked in a small army where there is no security. If the army is large and there is confidence that they will not be defeated and the disbelievers cannot dishonor the women, then there is no harm in taking women and the Qur'an along. But if it is a sariyah (small detachment) where there is fear that the women will be dishonored by the disbelievers, or the Qur'an will be dishonored, then taking them is not good. There is evidence for both cases in hadith. 'Abdullah ibn Umar reported that the Messenger of Allah prohibited traveling with the Qur'an to the enemy's land. In another hadith, the reason is given that the enemy might dishonor it.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage expands on a matn ruling about taking women and copies of the Qur'an (masahif) on military campaigns. The author states the general rule: if the army is large and secure, it is permissible (la ba's) to take them; but if it is a small detachment (sariyah) where security is not assured, it is makruh (disliked). The reasoning is to prevent dishonor to women and the Qur'an. The author then adduces two hadith: one from Bukhari and Muslim prohibiting travel with the Qur'an to enemy territory due to fear of desecration, and another hadith indicating that when security is assured, it is allowed. The commentary clarifies that the prohibition is conditional on fear of dishonor. This shows the Hanafi principle of maslaha: protecting the honor of women and the sanctity of the Qur'an takes precedence over the potential benefit of having them along. The author also includes marginal notes explaining some hadith references.

Key Terms
سریه (sariyah): A small military detachment sent on a raid, as opposed to a large army.
مصاحف (masahif): Plural of mus-haf, meaning copies of the Qur'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible to take women and copies of the Qur'an on a military campaign?
A) In any situation, because it is always beneficial.
B) Only when the army is large and there is no fear of desecration.
C) Only when the army is small and swift.
D) It is never permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school conditions the prohibition of taking the Qur'an to enemy territory on the fear of desecration; when security is assured, it is permissible.

6 min
Permission Requirement for Women and Slaves in Jihad
States ruling and exception with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women and slaves participating in jihad and identify when permission is required versus when it is not required.

Core Idea
Women need husband's permission; slaves need master's permission for offensive jihad, but defensive jihad requires no permission.

This lesson examines a specific fiqh ruling regarding who may participate in jihad. While jihad is a collective obligation, certain individuals—women and slaves—have restrictions due to their primary responsibilities. The Hanafi school, based on hadith evidence, distinguishes between offensive campaigns and defensive emergencies. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling in different scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Subjects
First, identify the act being ruled upon: women and slaves participating in jihad (fighting). Note the two categories: women and slaves. They share a similar ruling due to their status.
2
Distinguish Offensive vs Defensive Jihad
The ruling changes based on the type of jihad. In offensive jihad (normal campaigns), permission is required. In defensive jihad (when the enemy attacks), permission is not required because defense becomes obligatory on all.
This distinction is critical for understanding the exception.
3
State the Permission Requirement Rule and Exception
Formulate the rule: A woman may not fight without her husband's permission; a slave may not fight without his master's permission. The exception: unless the enemy attacks suddenly, then they may fight without permission for defense.
The exception is 'illa an yahjuma al-'aduw' (unless the enemy attacks).
4
Analyze the Hadith Evidence
Examine the hadith cited: the slave who followed the Prophet without his mistress's permission was ordered to return, and the Prophet stated that if he died before returning, his funeral prayer would not be offered. This indicates prohibition. Also the hadith about a man seeking permission for jihad: the Prophet told him to serve his parents, showing that permission from those with rights is needed.
The hadith on the slave is in Mustadrak al-Hakim; the hadith on parents is in Bukhari and Muslim.
5
Note the Qiyas for Women
The author explains that the ruling for women is derived by analogy (qiyas) from the slave hadith, and also from the hadith about parents, since a husband's rights are similar to parents' rights in requiring permission for jihad.
Qiyas is used to extend the ruling from slaves to women.

And it is disliked to take that out in a small detachment that is not secure. And a woman should not fight except with the permission of her husband, nor a slave except with the permission of his master, unless the enemy attacks. And when it is a large army, taking them is permissible; the evidence is this hadith... By analogy on this, taking a woman along is also considered. This is why at the Battle of Badr women were not taken because there was fear of dishonor, and at the Battle of Uhud women participated because that place was near Madinah. [12991] (12) A woman should not fight except with her husband's permission, nor a slave except with his master's permission, unless the enemy attacks. If the enemy attacks, then for self-defense and defense of the community, a woman may fight without her husband's permission, and a slave may fight without his master's permission. But in normal circumstances, a woman should not go for jihad without her husband's permission, nor a slave without his master's permission. The evidence for the slave not fighting without permission has passed in issue number 3: the hadith of al-Harith ibn Abdullah... From this hadith it is understood that in normal circumstances, it is not permissible for a slave to fight without his master's permission. And for a woman, we can make analogy on this issue. I heard Abdullah ibn Umar say: A man came to the Prophet seeking permission for jihad. He asked: Are your parents alive? He said yes. He said: Then in their service is your jihad.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage begins with a continuation of the previous lesson's topic, reiterating that taking women and Qur'an is makruh in a small detachment. Then it introduces a new ruling: a woman may not fight without her husband's permission, and a slave may not fight without his master's permission, except in the case of a sudden enemy attack (yuhjim al-'aduw). In such an emergency, they may fight without permission for defense. The author then provides evidence by first discussing the permissibility of taking women on a large campaign via qiyas (analogy) from the hadith about traveling with the Qur'an. He notes that at Badr women were not taken due to risk, but at Uhud they were because it was near Madinah. Then he provides two hadith: one about a slave who followed the Prophet without his mistress's permission, and the Prophet ordered him to return, stating that if he died before returning, his funeral prayer would not be offered. This shows the prohibition of fighting without permission. The second hadith is about a man seeking permission for jihad; the Prophet told him to serve his parents instead, indicating that permission from those with rights over you is required. By analogy, a wife needs her husband's permission. The commentary explains that in normal circumstances, the obligation of jihad is not absolute for women and slaves; their primary duties are to their husbands and masters respectively. However, if the enemy attacks, defense becomes obligatory for all, and permission is not required.

Key Terms
سید (sayyid): Master of a slave; the owner who has authority.
سرية (sariyah): A small military detachment sent on a mission.
اذن (idhn): Permission, consent.

Key Takeaway
Women require husband's permission and slaves require master's permission for offensive jihad, but defensive jihad removes this requirement.

Ruling on Women and Slaves in Jihad: Permission Required
Ruling with proofs for women and slaves in jihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women and slaves participating in jihad and identify the exception for defensive war, supported by the dalil from hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
A woman or slave cannot go to jihad without permission except for immediate self-defense.

This passage addresses a critical question: under what conditions may women and slaves engage in jihad? The Hanafi school establishes a default prohibition based on the rights of the husband and master, but allows participation in defensive situations. The author supports the ruling with prophetic hadith and analogical reasoning (qiyas), demonstrating how the school derives practical rulings from primary sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on women and slaves going out for jihad. The default ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) without permission, except when the enemy attacks (defensive war).
This is not a complete prohibition; permission or defense changes the ruling.
2
Locate the dalil for the slave
The author cites a hadith from al-Harith ibn Abdullah: a slave followed the Prophet to jihad, but the Prophet ordered him to return and seek his mistress's permission, warning that dying without returning would prevent his funeral prayer.
This hadith is from Mustadrak al-Hakim; transmission is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's explicit command to return indicates that permission is required; the severe consequence (no funeral prayer) shows the gravity of the prohibition.
The imperative 'ارجع' (return) in the context implies prohibition (tahrim).
4
Understand the qiyas for women
The ruling for women is derived by analogy (qiyas) to the slave ruling: both are under the authority of another (husband/master) and have duties that may conflict with jihad. Additionally, a hadith about parents (the son's jihad is serving his parents) provides further support by analogy: a woman's service to her husband is analogous.
The qiyas is based on the common illa (effective cause) of existing duties that take precedence over voluntary jihad.
5
Note the exception for defensive war
The passage explicitly states 'unless the enemy attacks' (الا ان يهجم العدو). In defensive situations, permission is waived because defense becomes an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) overriding normal restrictions.
This exception covers self-defense and community defense.

It is disliked to take the Quran on a small expedition where safety is not assured. (12991) A woman shall not fight except with her husband's permission, nor a slave except with his master's permission, unless the enemy attacks. If there is a large army, it is permissible to take it. The proof is this hadith: from Ibn Umar from the Prophet... This hadith shows that if there is security, the Quran can be taken. Based on this, we analogize taking women. This is why at Badr women were not taken because of fear, but at Uhud women participated because it was near Madinah. (12) A woman shall not fight except with her husband's permission, nor a slave except with his master's permission, unless the enemy attacks. In that case, for self-defense and defense of the community, a woman may fight without her husband's permission, and a slave without his master's permission. In normal circumstances, a woman should not go to jihad without her husband's permission, nor a slave without his master's permission. The proof for the slave is a hadith from al-Harith... From this hadith it is understood that generally a slave may not fight without his master's permission. And for women, we analogize. The hadith from Ibn Umar about the man who wanted to go to jihad shows that a son should not go without parents' permission because of the need for service; similarly, a woman should not go without her husband's permission because of the need for her service. Footnotes: (a) The Prophet and his companions traveled in enemy territory while learning the Quran. (b) The slave followed the Prophet to jihad, but the Prophet told him to return and inform his mistress, saying his example is like a slave who does not pray—if he dies, his funeral prayer will not be offered. (c) The Prophet told a man to serve his parents as that is his jihad.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Hanafi ruling on participation of women and slaves in jihad. It begins with the disliked nature of taking the Quran into enemy territory without safety, then moves to the core ruling: a woman may not fight without her husband's permission, nor a slave without his master's permission, except in defensive war when the enemy attacks. The author provides proof via hadith and qiyas (analogy). For women, the ruling is derived from the hadith about the slave, applying the same principle via qiyas. The hadith about parents serves as a further analogy: just as a son must serve his parents and cannot go to jihad without permission, a woman must serve her husband. The exceptions are limited to immediate defense. The passage emphasizes that in normal circumstances, these individuals are not obligated to jihad and may not participate without authorization.

Key Terms
سرية (sariyyah): A small military detachment sent by the main army.
جهاد (jihad): Striving in the path of Allah, specifically military expedition against non-believers.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case.

In the Hanafi school, women and slaves require permission for jihad except in immediate self-defense, derived from hadith and qiyas.

Prohibition on Killing Non-Combatants: The General Rule
Ruling with proof on the general prohibition of killing specified non-combatants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of non-combatants protected from killing in Hanafi law and cite the hadith from Ibn Umar as primary evidence.

Core Idea
Women, children, the very old, blind, and disabled are protected from killing in war unless they fight.

This lesson introduces the fundamental ruling that certain individuals are immune from killing in warfare. The Hanafi school relies on multiple authentic hadiths to establish this prohibition, which is a cornerstone of Islamic humanitarian law. We will examine the textual evidence and the legal reasoning behind each category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the six categories explicitly mentioned: woman, child, very old man (shaykh fani), blind person, disabled person (maq'ud). Note that the blind and disabled are added via qiyas or explicit texts.
The original text in Urdu adds 'paanch' (five) but it is likely a typo for 'maq'ud'.
2
Locate the primary hadith for women and children
The hadith from Ibn Umar (Bukhari and Muslim) is the clearest dalil: the Prophet explicitly forbade killing women and children. Read the exact wording.
Hadith text: 'Wajadtu imra'atan maqtulata... fainnahu naha 'an qatli al-nisa'i wa al-sibyan'.
3
Extend to other categories
The hadith from Anas ibn Malik (Abu Dawud) forbids killing a 'shaykh fani' (very old). The blind and disabled are analogized (qiyas) because they are equally unable to fight, or included by the general principle of non-combatancy.
Qiyas here is based on the shared illa of inability to participate in combat.
4
Understand the legal consequence
The imperative prohibition (nahy) indicates that killing such persons is haram unless an exception applies (covered in next lesson).
The exception is derived from the hadith of Samurah: 'uqtulu shuyukh al-mushrikin' narrowly interpreted.

Source Text

وينبغي للمسلمين ان لا يغدروا ولا يغلو ولا يمثلوا ولا يقتلوا امرأة ولا صبيا ولا شيخا فانيا ولا اعمى ولا مقعدا الا ان يكون احد هؤلاء ممن يكون له رأى في الحرب. مسلمانوں کے لئے مناسب ہے کہ دھوکا نہ کریں، نمبر ۱۷۴۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ عورت اور بچے کو قتل نہ کرے اور اس پر بہت بوڑھے کو قیاس کر لیں۔ ابوداؤد میں شیخ فانی کا لفظ بھی ہے۔ حدثني انس بن مالك ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال انطلقوا باسم ۲۶۱۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ بہت بوڑھے کو بھی قتل نہ کرے۔ البتہ اگر جنگ میں شرکت کرے یا جنگ میں رائے دے تو قتل کر سکتے ہیں اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن سمرة بن جندب قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اقتلوا شيوخ المشركين واستبقوا شرخهم (د) (ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی قتل النساء، ج ۲، ص ۶ نمبر ۲۶۷۰ ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی النزول علی الحکم بص ۲۸۷، نمبر ۱۵۸۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کفار کے بوڑھوں کو قتل کرو۔
It is appropriate for Muslims that they do not betray, nor act treacherously, nor mutilate, nor kill a woman, nor a child, nor a very old man, nor a blind person, nor a disabled person, unless one of these is among those who has an opinion in war. The proof for this is the hadith...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage states a ruling of the Hanafi school concerning the ethics of warfare. The ruling is that it is prohibited to kill certain categories of non-combatants: women, children, the very old, the blind, and the disabled. However, there is an exception: if any of these individuals actively participates in combat or gives military advice, they may be killed. The author supports this ruling with four hadiths from Sahih Muslim, Sunan Abi Dawud, Sahih Bukhari, and Sunan al-Tirmidhi. The first hadith (from Sulayman ibn Buraydah) includes the explicit prohibition of killing children (walid). The second (from Ibn Umar) specifically forbids killing women and children. The third (from Anas ibn Malik) adds the prohibition of killing the very old (shaykh fani). The fourth (from Samurah ibn Jundub) commands killing the elderly polytheists (shuyukh al-mushrikin) which is understood to refer to those who fight or advise, resolving the apparent contradiction.

Key Terms
مثلہ (muthlah): Mutilation of the dead or cutting of body parts such as nose and ears to disfigure.
غدر (ghadr): Breach of covenant or treachery, especially after giving assurance of safety.
شیخ فانی (shaykh fani): A very old man who is decrepit and unable to fight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The hadith of Ibn Umar that the Prophet forbade killing women and children
C) The hadith of Anas about not killing a very old man
D) The hadith of Samurah about killing elderly polytheists

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, women, children, the very old, blind, and disabled are protected from killing in war unless they actively participate in combat or give military advice.

The Exception for Combatant Non-Combatants
Ruling on the exception to the prohibition of killing non-combatants when they have a military role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exception to the rule of protecting non-combatants and cite the hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub as evidence, and explain how the Hanafi school reconciles the apparent contradiction between the hadiths.

Core Idea
Non-combatants who engage in combat or give military advice lose their protected status and may be lawfully killed.

The previous lesson established the general prohibition on killing six categories of non-combatants. However, this rule has an exception: if any of these individuals actively participates in fighting or provides strategic advice, the prohibition lifts. This exception is derived from the hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub. We will examine the evidence and the reasoning that resolves the apparent conflict with the general prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception clause
Read the phrase 'illa an yakuna ahad hadha'... this is the exception: unless one of them has an opinion (ra'y) in war or participates in combat.
The text gives two conditions: having an opinion/advice, or participating in fighting.
2
Locate the proof for the exception
The hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi) commands: 'uqtulu shuyukh al-mushrikin' (kill the elderly polytheists). This is the key evidence.
The hadith does not mention women, children, etc., so the exception is extended by qiyas to all protected categories when they act in a military capacity.
3
Reconcile with the general prohibition
The general prohibitions (hadiths of Ibn Umar, Anas, etc.) are absolute in their wording but must be restricted (takhsis) by the exception. The Hanafi method: the command in Samurah's hadith is specific to combatant elderly; the prohibition remains for non-combatants.
This is an application of the usul principle al-amm yuqayyad bi al-khass (the general is qualified by the specific).
4
Apply the exception logically
If a woman, child, blind, etc., engages in combat or gives advice, they fall under the same ruling as the elderly in Samurah's hadith, so killing them becomes permissible.
This is qiyas: the illa is active participation in war.

Source Text

The Exception of the Queen in Jihad
Exception to the prohibition: a queen may be killed because she directs the war

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a female queen in war and identify the two proofs used: the hadith on elderly polytheists and the precedent of a female attacker.

Core Idea
A queen who commands the enemy may be killed, for she is the cause of war – a greater reason than an elderly polytheist.

Islamic law generally prohibits killing women in war. However, this general rule has exceptions. One key exception, affirmed by the Hanafi school, is when a woman holds command authority – specifically a queen (malikah) who directs the enemy forces. This lesson examines the ruling and the evidence that supports it, particularly the hadith commanding the killing of elderly polytheists and the principle of qiyas awla (greater reason).

Guided Path
1
State the general prohibition and its exception
Begin by recalling the general rule: women and children are not to be killed in war. The exception: a queen who commands the enemy is liable to be killed because she is the cause of war.
This is a ruling with proof, not a mere statement.
2
Identify the first proof – the hadith on elderly polytheists
The passage cites the hadith: 'Kill the elderly polytheists and spare their children.' This is an ahad hadith, but the Hanafi school accepts it as evidence in non-essential matters. The queen is killed by greater reason (awla) because she holds a higher rank than the elderly.
Note the dalil: the command to kill shuyukh is general, and the queen is included by qiyas awla.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah of the hadith
The operative word is 'uqtulu' (kill). It indicates permissibility and even recommendation. The illa (effective cause) is being a cause of war or a commander. Since the queen is the ultimate commander, she is more deserving of being killed than an elderly person who may only be a participant.
The Hanafi school uses the general command and extends it via illa.
4
Examine the second proof – the precedent of a female attacker
The passage refers to a known incident where a woman tried to kill a Muslim fighter and was killed in return. The queen, who causes killing through her army, is analogous.
This reinforces the principle that a woman who directly contributes to warfare loses her protected status.
5
Determine the scope of the ruling
Apply the ruling: any woman who effectively commands the enemy forces, regardless of title, may be killed. The Hanafi position is that a queen is killed simply by virtue of her command role, without needing to personally fight.
Note the cross-madhhab difference: some schools require personal fighting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۹۳] (۱۴) او تكون المرأة مَلِكَة. عورت رانی ہو تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ قتال کرانے کی پوری ذمہ داری عورت ہی ہے اس لئے وہ قتل کی جائے گی۔ وہی قتال کا سبب ہے (۲) اوپر حدیث گزری۔ عن سمرة بن جندب قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اقتلوا شيوخ المشركين واستبقوا شرخهم (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی قتل النساء، ج ۲، ص ۶ نمبر ۲۶۷۰ ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی النزول علی الحکم بص ۲۸۷، نمبر ۱۵۸۳) اس حدیث میں ہے مشرکین کے شیوخ کو قتل کرو، اور رانی تو شیوخ سے بھی بڑا عہدہ رکھتی ہے اس لئے وہ بدرجہ اولی قتل کی جائے گی (۳) اوپر بھی ایک حدیث گزری کہ ایک عورت نے مجاہد کو قتل کرنے کی کوشش کی تو وہ عورت قتل کی گئی۔ جب رانی فوج کے ذریعہ قتل کروارہی ہے تو وہ بھی قتل کی جائے گی۔
Or if the woman is a queen. If the woman is a queen, it means the entire responsibility of causing war lies upon her; therefore she will be killed. She is the cause of war. The hadith cited above: Samurah ibn Jundub reported that the Prophet said: 'Kill the elderly polytheists and spare their children.' This hadith commands killing the elderly polytheists, and a queen holds an even higher rank than the elderly, so she is killed with greater reason. Also, the hadith about a woman who tried to kill a fighter was cited above; she was killed. Since a queen causes killing through her army, she too will be killed.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage addresses the specific case of a woman who is a queen (malikah) leading the enemy. The general prohibition against killing women in war does not apply to her because she is the commander and the cause of hostilities. The reasoning uses two arguments: first, analogy from the hadith that commands killing elderly polytheists (shuyukh) — since the queen is of higher status, she is more deserving of being killed (awla). Second, the precedent of a particular woman who tried to kill a Muslim fighter and was killed in return. The queen is responsible for ordering the army, so she is akin to a combatant. Hanafi scholars extend this ruling to any woman who participates in warfare, whether through direct fighting or command.

Key Terms
ملکہ (malikah): A female ruler or queen who commands the enemy forces.
بدرجہ اولی (bi-darajat awla): By greater reason; a qiyas awla argument: if a lower case is included, the higher case is logically included.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is an adult
B) Because she holds command responsibility and is the cause of war
C) Because she is wealthy
D) Because she is a polytheist

Key Takeaway
A queen commanding the enemy is an exception to the prohibition of killing women in war, proven by qiyas awla from the hadith on elderly polytheists.

The Prohibition of Killing an Insane Person in War
Analogical ruling linking insane person to child via shared lack of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing an insane person in war and explain the qiyas jali linking it to the prohibition against killing children.

Core Idea
An insane person in war is like a child: both lack intellect, so killing either is haram.

The previous lesson established from hadith that children are not to be killed in war. Here, the commentator extends that ruling to insane persons through clear analogical reasoning (qiyas jali). Let's explore why this analogy holds and its implications for the ethics of jihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl) and its ruling
The asl is a child; the ruling is that killing a child in war is not permissible (haram), based on the hadith 'Do not kill a child' (لا تقتلوا وليدا).
This hadith is taken as a primary evidence.
2
Identify the new case (far')
The far' is an insane person (majnoon) who lacks intellectual capacity.
The passage explicitly links the two by stating 'just as a child has no intellect, likewise an insane person has no intellect'.
3
Extract the effective cause (illa)
The illa is the lack of intellect (aql) and the consequent inability to meaningfully participate in warfare. This is shared by both the child and the insane person.
This is a jaliyyah (obvious) illa because it is directly apparent from the description.
4
Apply the illa to derive the ruling for the far'
Since the illa of lacking intellect is present in both, the ruling of prohibition transfers from the child to the insane person.
Thus, killing an insane person is haram unless an exception applies (e.g., if they actively engage in fighting).
5
Note any exceptions or conditions
The mufta bihi position includes an exception: if the insane person actively participates in combat, they may become a legitimate target, similar to children who fight.
This exception prevents the ruling from being absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۹۴] (۱۵) ولا يقتلوا مجنونا. جس طرح بچے کو عقل نہیں ہوتی اسی طرح مجنون کو عقل نہیں ہوتی۔ اور بچے کو قتل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے تو مجنون کو بھی قتل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔
And do not kill an insane person. Just as a child has no intellect, likewise an insane person has no intellect. And killing a child is not permissible, so killing an insane person is also not permissible.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage provides a straightforward qiyas (analogical reasoning) within the Hanafi school. The prohibition against killing children is established by the hadith evidence in the previous lesson. Here, the commentator extends that ruling to an insane person (majnoon) because the effective cause ('illa) is the lack of intellectual capacity (aql). Since both the child and the insane person lack the ability to meaningfully participate in combat or understand the warfare, killing them is prohibited. This is a clear example of qiyas jali (obvious analogy). The ruling is part of the ethics of jihad: only those who can fight or give counsel are legitimate targets.

Key Terms
مجنون (majnoon): An insane person who lacks sound intellect, similar to a child in mental capacity.
قیاس جلی (qiyas jali): Obvious analogy where the effective cause is directly apparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is an insane person likened to a child in the ruling on killing in war?
A) Both are physically weak
B) Both lack intellect and are not responsible for warfare
C) Both belong to the same tribe
D) Both are minors

Key Takeaway
Killing an insane person in war is haram by analogy to children, both lacking intellect; the exception is if they actively engage in combat.

Rules on Killing Non-Combatants in War
Rulings on which enemy combatants may be killed, with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on which non-combatants may be killed in war and explain the proofs from hadith and analogy.

Core Idea
Non-combatants are protected except when they actively contribute to war.

In Islamic warfare, the default rule is that non-combatants such as children, the elderly, the blind, the disabled, and the insane are not to be killed. However, this ruling has exceptions: if any of these individuals directly participates in fighting or provides strategic advice, they may be targeted. This lesson examines the Hanafi position, its scriptural proofs, and the analogical reasoning used to extend rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default prohibition
The passage lists categories of people who are generally not to be killed: child, decrepit old man, blind, disabled, insane woman (unless fighting or a queen). Recognize that the default ruling is haram (forbidden) for these categories.
2
Recognize the exceptions
Note the exceptions: if the person has a war opinion (i.e., gives strategic advice) or if the woman is a queen (i.e., ruler ordering the war), they may be killed. Understand that these exceptions are based on their active role in the conflict.
3
Examine the hadith proofs
The commentator cites two hadiths: (1) The Prophet's prohibition of killing women, with an exception when a woman tried to kill a Muslim soldier; (2) The command to kill old men of polytheists but spare young boys. Understand how these establish the general rule and exceptions.
4
Apply qiyas (analogy)
The prohibition of killing children is extended to the insane by analogy because both lack reason and are not combatants. Identify the illa (effective cause) as lack of intellect and inability to fight.

A child, a decrepit old man, a blind person, and a disabled person [are not killed] unless one of these has a war opinion, or the woman is a queen. And they do not kill an insane person. And if a woman participates in war, the proof for killing her is this hadith: 'Ikrimah narrated that the Prophet saw a dead woman in Ta'if and said: Did I not forbid the killing of women? Because Islam does not allow shedding blood without reason. [2993] (14) Or that the woman is a queen. If the woman is a queen, it means she is fully responsible for causing war, so she is killed. She is the cause of fighting. (2) The hadith mentioned earlier: 'Samurah ibn Jundub said: The Messenger of Allah said: Kill the old men of the polytheists and spare their young boys.' In this hadith, the old men of the polytheists are killed, and a queen holds an even higher position than the old men, so she is killed with even more reason. (3) Also the hadith mentioned earlier about the woman who tried to kill the mujahid, so she was killed. When a queen is causing killing through her army, she is also killed. Sharakh: young boy. [2994] (15) And do not kill an insane person. Just as a child has no intellect, similarly an insane person has no intellect. And since killing a child is not permissible, killing an insane person is also not permissible. I had seated her behind me, and she tried to throw me off and kill me. So the Prophet ordered that she be buried. (b) The Prophet said: Kill the old men of the polytheists and spare their young boys. From Samurah ibn Jundub, the Prophet said: Kill the old men of the polytheists and spare their young boys. In this hadith, do not kill children, and by analogy, it is not permissible to kill an insane person.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding whom to kill among the enemy non-combatants. The base ruling is that children, decrepit old men, blind, disabled, and insane persons are not to be killed in war, as they are not considered combatants. However, there are exceptions: if any of these individuals has a war opinion (i.e., they provide strategic advice to the enemy) or if a woman is a queen (i.e., a ruler who orders the war), then they may be killed because they are effectively causing the war. The commentator provides two hadith proofs: one from Sunan (the story of the woman at Ta'if) showing that a woman who actively tries to kill a Muslim soldier can be killed, and another from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud regarding killing the old men of the polytheists. The commentator explains that the general principle is that only those who directly fight or are capable of fighting are to be killed. The word 'shuyukh' (old men) in the hadith refers to those who participate in war or give advice, not all elderly. Similarly, a queen is even more responsible than an old man, so she is killed. The prohibition against killing children is extended by qiyas (analogy) to the insane because they lack reason just as children do. The commentator also defines difficult terms: yandar (treachery), yakhlū (embezzlement), muthlah (mutilation), muq'ad (disabled).

Key Terms
شيوخ (shuyukh): Old men among the polytheists who actively participate in war or give strategic advice.
شرخ (sharkh): Young boys, children who are not yet mature or capable of fighting.
مثلہ (muthlah): Mutilation of a dead body by cutting off body parts to disfigure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly limits killing in war to those who are combatants or directly contribute to the war effort, protecting all other non-combatants.

The Obligation to Notify Before Breaking a Treaty
States ruling on notification before breaking treaty and exception when enemy initiates treachery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that notification before breaking a treaty is wajib and identify the exception when the enemy initiates treachery.

Core Idea
Notification is wajib before breaking a treaty; only enemy treachery exempts from notification.

This lesson addresses a key principle of Islamic international law: the sanctity of treaties and the requirement of fair notice before abrogation. The Hanafi school derives this from the Quranic command 'fanbidh ilayhim 'ala sawa'' and the prophetic hadith. An exception exists when the enemy itself first betrays the agreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its basis
State the general rule: breaking a treaty requires prior notification to the enemy. The ruling is derived from the Quranic verse 8:58 and the hadith of Amr ibn Abasa.
The imperative 'fanbidh' indicates obligation (wajib) in Hanafi usul.
2
Examine the exception for enemy treachery
If the enemy breaches the treaty first (by mutual agreement), no notification is required; Muslims may fight immediately. This is based on the incident of Banu Nufatha and the conquest of Mecca.
The exception prevents dishonoring the treaty; the enemy has already nullified it.
3
Apply to historical examples
Consider the case of Muawiya and the Romans: the treaty was honored until expiration. Contrast with the case of Quraysh aiding Banu Bakr against Banu Ka'b, where treachery justified immediate action.
These examples illustrate the practical application of the rule.
4
Identify the ethical foundation
The obligation to notify upholds the Quranic principle that Allah does not love the treacherous. Notification ensures fairness and avoids deceit.
Even when breaking a treaty, Muslims must act honorably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انفع نبذ اليهم وقاتلهم (1997) (18) فان بدأوا بخيانة قاتلهم ولم ينبذ اليهم اذا كان ذلك باتفاقهم. واما تخافن من قوم خيانة فانبد اليهم على سواء ان الله لا يحب الخائنين. سمعت سليم بن عامر يقول كان بين معاوية وبين اهل الروم عهد وكان يسير في بلادهم حتى اذا انقضى العهد اغار عليهم فاذا رجل على دابة او على فرس وهو يقول الله اكبر وفاء لا غدر [؟] واذاهو عمرو بن عبسة فسأله معاوية عن ذلك فقال سمعت رسول الله يقول من كان بينه وبين قوم عهد فلا يحلن عهدا و لا يشدنه حتى يمضى آمده او ينبذ اليهم على سواء قال فرجع معاوية بالناس. وحديث عروة بمعناه قال ثم ان بني نفاثة من بني الديل اغاروا على بني كعب وهم في المدة التي بين رسول الله وبين قريش وكان بنو كعب في صلح رسول الله وكان بنو نفاثة في صلح قريش فاعانت بنو بكر بني نفاثة واعانتهم قريش بالسلاح والرقيق فذكر القصة قال فخرج ركب من بني كعب حتى الونا رسول الله فذكروا له الذي اصابهم وماكان من قريش عليهم في ذلك، ثم ذكر قصة خروج رسول الله الى مكة.
It is beneficial to throw to them and fight them (1997)(18). If they begin with treachery, fight them and do not throw to them if that is by their agreement. And if you fear treachery from a people, throw to them on equal terms; indeed Allah does not love the treacherous. I heard Sulaym ibn Amir say: There was a treaty between Muawiya and the people of Rome, and he was traveling in their land until when the treaty expired, he attacked them. Then a man on a mount or a horse said: Allahu Akbar, fulfillment not treachery. And he was Amr ibn Abasa. Muawiya asked him about that, and he said: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: Whoever has a treaty with a people, let him not dissolve the treaty nor strengthen it until its term expires or he throws to them on equal terms. He said: So Muawiya returned with the people. And the hadith of Urwa with its meaning: He said: Then Banu Nufatha of Banu al-Dil attacked Banu Ka'b while they were in the period between the Messenger of Allah and Quraysh. Banu Ka'b were in the treaty of the Messenger of Allah, and Banu Nufatha were in the treaty of Quraysh. Banu Bakr aided Banu Nufatha, and Quraysh aided them with weapons and slaves. He mentioned the story. He said: A group from Banu Ka'b went out until they came to the Messenger of Allah and mentioned to him what had happened to them and what Quraysh had done against them. Then he mentioned the story of the Messenger of Allah's departure to Mecca.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the book of Siyar. It begins by stating the general rule: if it is beneficial to break a treaty and fight the enemy, one may do so, but only after notifying the enemy. This is derived from the Quranic verse (8:58) which commands throwing the treaty back to them on equal terms if treachery is feared. The hadith of Amr ibn Abasa confirms that the treaty must be honored until its term or until notification is given. Then the passage addresses an exception: if the enemy themselves initiate treachery (i.e., break the treaty by agreement among themselves), then it is permissible to fight without prior notification. This is supported by the incident of the Banu Nufatha attacking Banu Ka'b, which led to the conquest of Mecca without prior notice because Quraysh had violated the treaty. The practical consequence is that notification is required as a general principle to avoid treachery, but not when the enemy has already breached the agreement.

Key Terms
نبذ (nabdh): To formally cancel a treaty by notification.
عهد ('ahd): A binding agreement with non-Muslims.
خیانة (khiyanah): Breach of trust or treaty violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) When the Muslims are in a position of strength
D) When the enemy is a non-Muslim state

Key Takeaway
Before breaking a treaty, notification is obligatory; the only exception is when the enemy has already betrayed the agreement.

Enemy Slaves Seeking Refuge Become Free
States that enemy slaves who come to Muslim camp become free.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on enemy slaves who enter the Muslim camp and explain the evidence from the hadith of Ali.

Core Idea
Enemy slaves who reach the Muslim camp are free unconditionally.

This lesson covers a specific rule of Siyar: enemy slaves who come to the Muslim camp gain freedom. The ruling is based on a hadith from Ali (RA) about two slaves who came to the Prophet at Hudaybiyyah. Understanding this principle helps clarify the Hanafi approach to manumission in the context of war and the priority of freedom over property rights of the enemy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Start with the explicit statement: 'And if their slaves come out to the Muslim camp, they are free.' The key term is that they become free upon arrival.
The ruling is unconditional according to the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the proof
The proof is a hadith from Ali. Note the context: two slaves came to the Prophet before the treaty of Hudaybiyyah. Their masters demanded return, claiming the slaves fled from slavery, not faith. The Prophet refused and declared them 'freed ones of Allah.'
This hadith is the primary textual evidence for the ruling.
3
Analyze the dalala
The Prophet's refusal to return the slaves and his statement 'hum 'utaqa' Allah' indicate that they are legally free. The imperative to treat them as free is derived from this action and words.
The hadith has zanni thubut but qati dalala for this ruling.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school conditions that the slave must be Muslim to be freed. The Hanafi school does not require this, as the hadith does not mention the slaves' faith, and the principle of encouraging freedom prevails.
The practical difference: Hanafis free all enemy slaves; Shafi'is only Muslim ones.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, when enemy slaves reach the Muslim camp voluntarily, they are to be treated as free persons. Muslims may not return them to their former masters.
This ruling applies to harbi slaves (from enemy territory).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا خرج عبيدهم الى عسكر المسلمين فهم احرار. عن على قال خرج عبدان الى رسول الله يعنى يوم الحديبية قبل الصلح فكتب اليه مواليهم فقالوا يا محمد ! والله ما خرجوا اليك رغبة في دينك وانما خرجوا هربا من الرق ، فقال ناس صدقوا يا رسول الله ردهم اليهم، فغضب رسول الله وقال ما اراكم تنتهون يا معشر قريش حتى يبعث الله عليكم من يضرب رقابكم على هذا وابى ان يردهم وقال هم عتقاء الله عز وجل.
And if their slaves come out to the Muslim camp, they are free. From Ali: He said: Two slaves came out to the Messenger of Allah on the day of Hudaybiyyah before the treaty. Their masters wrote to him and said: O Muhammad, by Allah, they did not come out to you out of desire for your religion, but they came out fleeing from slavery. Some people said: They are truthful, O Messenger of Allah; return them. The Messenger of Allah became angry and said: I see you will not stop, O people of Quraysh, until Allah sends upon you someone who will strike your necks for this. And he refused to return them and said: They are the freed ones of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the book of Siyar: if slaves of the enemy (non-Muslims) come to the Muslim military camp, they become free. The basis is the incident of two slaves who came to the Prophet at Hudaybiyyah. Despite their masters claiming they fled due to slavery, the Prophet refused to return them and declared them freed for the sake of Allah. This indicates that the right to manumission overrides the property rights of the enemy in the context of war. The Hanafi position is that such slaves become free immediately upon reaching the Muslim camp due to the general principle of encouragement of manumission.

Key Terms
عبيد ('abid): Slaves (plural of 'abd).
عتقاء (utaqa'): Freed ones (plural of 'atiq).
حرية (hurriyyah): Freedom, the status of being free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) They are sold

Key Takeaway
Enemy slaves who voluntarily enter the Muslim camp are legally free and cannot be returned; this is based on the Prophetic precedent and the Hanafi emphasis on manumission.
